These materials can be in general non-conductive polymers such as PVP and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF), to enhance polarization losses.645,701,709–711 Conductive materials including carbon nanotubes,648,712 carbon nanofibers,296,642 graphene,650,713 and polypyrrole,714 are also commonly used to combine with 1D magnetic colloidal chains to improve their permeability-permittivity matching (Fig. 26b). Their minimal reflection loss can be reduced to −63 dB when coating a carbon layer on ferrite chains,296 and the absorption bandwidth with a reflection loss less than −10 dB can reach 14.8 GHz by embedding Fe/Fe3C chains in carbon nanotubes.648 These radiation absorption properties are remarkably enhanced comparing to non-composite magnetic colloidal chains.
 Fig. 26  1D colloidal chains applied for the absorption of electromagnetic waves. (a–a′) Scheme and TEM image of 1D chains with yolk–shell structure and their radiation absorption performance. (b–b′) Scheme and TEM image of FeCo/PVDF core–shell nanochains@graphene oxide composites and their electromagnetic wave-absorbing bandwidth. (c–c′) Scheme and SEM image of PVDF/MXene/Ni chain composite films and their electromagnetic interference shielding effectiveness (EMI SE) with different film thicknesses. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 296 Copyright 2018, Tsinghua University Press and Springer; (b) ref. 701 Copyright 2021, Wiley-VCH; (c) ref. 649 Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH. 
A recently emerging conductive material worth emphasizing in the radiation absorption field is MXene. MXene is a booming family of 2D transition metal carbides and nitrides, showing efficient electron transport and dipole polarization properties.715 When compositing MXene with Ni chains and PVDF, the shielding effectiveness of the resulting composite can be up to 19.3 and 34.4 dB with a film thickness of only 0.10 and 0.36 mm, respectively (Fig. 26c).649 A Mxene/Co nanochains composite shows a minimal reflection loss of −46.48 dB with a thickness of 1.02 mm.716 Almost all current research of applying 1D nanochains to radiation absorption focus on light weight, impedance matching, and high electromagnetic wave absorption. When 1D colloidal chains come into practical applications, many more complicated factors, for instance, mechanical properties and durability have to be taken into consideration. And sometimes the radiation absorption property needs to be partially sacrificed as a compromise.


3.2.3 Catalysts. 
Excellent catalytic activity, selectivity, long term stability, recyclability and reusability are basic characteristics that must be taken into consideration when designing a catalyst. Commonly used strategies to improve the stability and catalytic activity of catalysts are endowing them with good dispersibility against aggregation, and increasing the number of active facets exposed to a substrate, respectively. 1D colloidal chains can be a versatile platform to immobilize tiny, nanoscale catalysts, thus preventing the catalyst from aggregation.428,715 Either loaded on existing 1D chains58,717,718 or catalysts themselves assembling into colloidal chains,719 the 1D chain-like structures provide more catalytically active sites and hot spots.720
Beside loading catalysts, 1D colloidal chains are also able to accelerate catalytic reactions from the aspects of charge transfer and mass transport. Conductive 1D structures made of carbon nanotubes,721–723 nanofibers,724 metal NPs,520,725–727 and their interlaced 3D networks721,728–730 serve as channels for directional flow of electrons. This facilitates charge transfer during catalytic reactions, thus, promoting the reaction kinetics (Fig. 27a). While the introduction of porous and hollow structures into 1D colloidal chains enhances mass transport and the availability of catalysts for substrates.89,731–733 Actually, the colloidal chains themselves could induce enhancement in chain center-of-mass diffusivity because of phoretic hydrodynamic flows and Brownian fluctuations, and this enhancement is found stronger in flexible chains.57 A more extensive approach to accelerating mass transfer is endowing catalyst-loaded 1D colloidal chains with magnetics properties, as this allows to use magnetic chains as micro stirrer to homogenize the catalysis solution under a rotating magnetic field. Metal NPs,142,312,732,734–737 quantum dots,738 enzymes739 and other catalysts740 have been introduced to such magnetic micro stirrers (Fig. 27b). The magnetic responsiveness also favors a quick recycling of 1D catalysts, leading to an improved reusability.
 Fig. 27  1D colloidal chains as catalysts. (a) Scheme of preparation of Co@Co9S8 chains, and their enhanced charge transfer resulting from 3D networks composed of conductive Co@Co9S8 colloidal chains. Such enhanced charge transfer reduces the overpotential of oxygen evolution. (b) Scheme of magnetotactic bacteria (AMB-1) used as micro stirrers to enhance the epoxidation of cyclooctene catalyzed by phosphotungstic acid in Pickering emulsion. Comparing to Pickering emulsion without AMB-1 stirrers or normal stirrers, AMB-1 can largely promote the conversion. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 725 Copyright 2018, ACS; (b) the term of Creative Commons Attribution 3.0 Unported Licence, ref. 278 Copyright 2018, the authors, published by RSC. 
Sometimes, more than one component is needed to catalyze a tandem reaction. 1D colloidal chains are an outstanding platform to integrate and glue multiple components together to perform more complex catalytic reactions.79,255,739,741,742 It has to be pointed out that in an excellent 1D catalyst, enhanced mass/charge transfer, multiple active sites, recyclability, and the synergistic effect of multiple components need to be comprehensively balanced, rather than focusing on only one of the aspects.735,743


3.2.4 Energy materials. 
Energy conversion and storage materials are hot topics in material science and engineering. Even though it is unrealistic to hope that 1D colloidal chain can deal with all challenges in this field, the anisotropic structure and its emergent and enhanced collective properties still provide potential solutions to selected problems encountered in current energy materials. For instance, matching the absorption spectrum of a photoelectrode in a solar cell with the solar radiation spectrum is a crucial way to improve the power conversion efficiency.744,745 Strong electronic coupling between nanocrystals in colloidal chains leads to a tunable alteration of the optical band gaps (Fig. 28a),197 which may better match the solar radiation spectrum.
 Fig. 28  1D colloidal chains used as energy materials. (a) Optical band gap tuned by inter-nanocrystal distance of 1D Perovskite chains, the structures with smaller inter-nanocrystal spacing show a smaller optical band gap. (b–b′′) Scheme and TEM image of a sulfur loaded ZIF-8@carbon nanotube network, and their cycling performance when used as electrodes for sodium-ion batteries. (c–c′) Scheme and SEM image of 1D Zn–Co–S yolk–shell chains embedded in a carbon nanotube/graphene film, and (c′′) their Ragone plot comparison with other materials. (d–d′′) Scheme and TEM image of MoS2 encapsulated in necklaced nanotubes (ANL MoS2@CNFs) to alleviate the volume expansion, and their cycling performance when used in lithium/sodium-ion batteries. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 197 Copyright 2019, ACS; (b) ref. 746 Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH; (c) ref. 747 Copyright 2020, ACS; (d) ref. 748 Copyright 2020, RSC. 
Actually, 1D colloidal chains are more commonly applied as electrode materials for energy storage capacitors and lithium/sodium-ion batteries than applied in solar energy conversion. Typical capacitor materials, for example, transition metal oxides, feature lower energy storage capability than their theoretical capacity because of their poor intrinsic conductivity.313,749 Assembling capacitor materials, or co-assembling them with other conductive components such as CoB alloy and carbon nanofibers into 1D colloidal chains largely improves their practical capacitance.88,313,314,747,750,751
Similar to 1D chains in catalysts, charge transfer has also to be taken into consideration when using 1D colloidal chains as energy materials because electron/ion transport affects the high-rate performance of lithium/sodium-ion batteries.752 Therefore, electrode materials including LiCoO2,753 Fe3O4,297 FeS2754 and Au–Pt755 have been assembled into 1D colloidal chains, or integrated with carbon nanotubes746,756,757 and carbon nanofibers.758–760 These 1D structures further form 3D interconnected, electrically conductive networks to facilitate charge transfer (Fig. 28b). An additional advantage brought by 1D structures is that these materials can be processed to freestanding electrodes without binders, thus increasing the capacity density.305,757,758 Porous structures are also introduced to 1D colloidal chains to shorten the diffusion distance of electrons/ions, to improve exchange kinetics during charge-discharge cycles, and to expose an increasing number of electroactive sites (Fig. 28c).305,364,747,751,754,761
A widespread challenge existing in lithium/sodium-ion batteries is volumetric expansion of electrode materials in the course of lithium/sodium insertion/extraction. Loading nanoscale active materials into/onto 1D colloidal chains to downsize the dimensions of active components,743,759,762 and coating them with another material (usually a layer of carbon film) to suppress the expansion748,763 are two main strategies coupled with 1D colloidal chains to address this challenge (Fig. 28d). Introducing lithiophilic components such as titanium nitride764 and silver765,766 to smoothly deposit lithium/sodium on 1D structures, has been applied to protect electrodes from dendrites and solve short-circuiting problems brought by dendrites.


3.2.5 Biomedical applications. 
Cytotoxicity is a key aspect before putting any material into biomedical applications. Unfortunately, 1D structures with high length-diameter ratio typically reduce cell viability due to damage to the cell membrane as compared to spherical counterparts of the same material.767,768 Therefore, relatively short chains are always used within a cellular context, and a layer of biocompatible polymer,769 carbon770 or SiO2771 is coated on such chains to reduce their cytotoxicity. On the other hand, the cytotoxicity of long 1D colloidal chains can also be applied, for example to kill microbes (Fig. 29a).772 One more interesting property of 1D structure is its longer blood circulation than spherical particles,767,773 which is a dominant advantage when 1D nanochains are utilized to treat blood diseases such as leukemia (Fig. 29b)774 or to highly sensitively detect signal molecules in blood to help early disease diagnosis and prognosis.775
 Fig. 29  1D colloidal chains for biomedical applications. (a) Scheme and TEM image of the interactions of FeO NP@C nanochains with E. coli, and (a′) comparing to the magnetic nanochains without wash steps after each treatment, the nanochains with wash steps have higher loss of E. coli viability. (b) Scheme and TEM image of vincristine-loaded superparamagnetic anisotropic nano-assemblies (VCR-SANs). The VCR-SANs have a much longer mean residence time (MRT) in plasma compared to VCR and VCR-loaded isotropic nano-assemblies (VCR-SINs). (c) Compared to monodisperse spheres (S-MNPs-PPR) and clusters (C-MNPs-PPR) of Mn–Zn ferrite loading Cyclo(RGDyK) (PPR), 1D worm-like structures (W-MNPs-PPR) possess a higher relaxivity coefficient (r2), therefore show better negative contrast effects. (d–d′) Scheme of polydopamine-coated Au NPs and nanochains, and the comparison of intracellular dark field scattering images of HeLa cells incubated for 24 h with unassembled NPs and nanochains. (e) Scheme of Au NP chains coated with SiO2 and the H2O2-induced shift of chain plasmon resonance. (e′) Combined with laser radiation, such Au NP chains can effectively reduce the relative tumor volume, showing better tumor-microenvironment-activated theranostics behavior. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 772 Copyright 2014, ACS; (b) ref. 774 Copyright 2016, RSC; (c) ref. 776 Copyright 2019, ACS; (d) ref. 777 Copyright 2019, ACS; (e) ref. 778 Copyright 2021, Wiley-VCH. 
In addition to utilizing the two above-mentioned characteristics of 1D structures, enhancement in magnetization of 1D magnetic colloidal chains, and red shift of longitudinal surface plasmon resonance (LSPR) of 1D noble metal nanochains are two other categories of collective properties, which are most extensively used for biomedical applications. The increased magnetization and local magnetic field strength of 1D magnetic nanochains drive surrounding protons to transversely relax faster than protons around discrete magnetic particles. This results in an improved contrast ability of T2-weighted magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).770 The enhanced magnetization also leads to a faster magnetic responsiveness776 and a higher efficiency of magnetocaloric effect under an alternating magnetic field.275 All these enhanced effects together make Fe3O4, Fe2O3, Mn–Zn ferrite and Co based 1D magnetic colloidal chains an ideal platform for in vivo MRI (Fig. 29c), targeted drug delivery, ultrafast detection and separation of cells and proteins,775,779 and magnetic hyperthermia treatment of diseases such as inflammation and cancer.275,776,780–784 Magnetic nanochains have also worked as micro stirrers to generate shear force inside cells to inhibit cell differentiation under a rotating magnetic field.785
As to 1D noble metal colloidal chains, they can be designed such that the wavelength of their emerging LSPR red shifts to the second near-infrared (NIR-II) window. Combined with deeper penetration depth, lower optical absorption and scattering from biological tissue within NIR-II,786 Ag or Au NP chains with their multiple hot spots of plasmon coupling have served as an efficient photothermal agent for photoacoustic imaging (Fig. 29d), photothermal therapy and as photothermal bactericide.771,777,787–791
Additionally, 1D colloidal chains with multi-binding sites exhibit more binding opportunities to cell membranes compared to their spherical variants,775,777 which is especially useful for targeting low abundant tumor cells and delivering drugs for early diagnosis and treatment.575,792–794 Beside integrating multiple functions such as diagnosis and treatment into a signal chain, the other current focus of 1D colloidal chains for biomedical applications is endowing the platform with responsive and intelligent features. For instance, discrete Au NPs confined in a 1D SiO2 pocket only come in close contact to form Au NP chains when H2O2 is present in a tumor microenvironment. Such 1D Au NP chains show strong NIR-II absorption, which can convert light to heat for tumor-microenvironment-activated theranostics (Fig. 29e).778

4. Conclusions and outlook
In this review, we have comprehensively summarized how low dimensional building blocks can be organized to construct various 1D colloidal chains. The architectures of the 1D chains range from simple single component chains with specific orientation, via bi-/multi-component block chains, to alternating and sequence-controlled structures with defined length, width, interparticle distance, chirality and bundles. These 1D chains can be further assembled to higher hierarchical arrangements such as 2D arrays and 3D assemblies. The underlying strategies to assemble these 1D anisotropic structures mainly fall into two categories. In the first category chains are assembled from isotropic building blocks under directional guidance including field-, template- and ligand-directed assembly. The other category includes chains assembled from shape-/surface-anisotropic building blocks under non-directional force on nano/micro scales. Using the former strategy, except for DNA and confining templates, colloidal chains can be synthesized on a relatively large scale, but with a relatively low competence in controlling the sequence, length, and interparticle distance of the chains. The latter strategy shows better sequence controllability and reversibility of the chains, but lacks the ability to restrict their geometry. DNA and confining templates are able to assemble colloidal chains with precisely defined geometry and hierarchical parameters. Unfortunately, DNA and confining templates also face challenges in the aspect of scalable production. This limits them to the development of synthetic methodologies and exploring emergent and enhanced collective properties, but prevents them to some extent from practical real-life applications. Overall, the challenges and opportunities in terms of preparation of colloidal chains are to develop scalable, practical procedures to assemble 1D colloidal structures with specific orientation, particular sequence and precisely controlled geometric construction. This will lay a solid foundation for following research in properties and applications.
Colloidal chains share very similar growth mechanisms with polymerization in polymer science. Learning new concepts and methods in polymer science, for instance, controlled/“living” radical polymerization795 and sequence controlled polymerization,796 may generate new approaches to controlling the primary structure of colloidal chains. Biological processes in nature, such as protein and DNA/RNA synthesis, protein and DNA folding, may be used as inspiration for assembling nanochains into secondary and tertiary architectures with higher level complexity and hierarchies. Given that current 1D colloidal chains with relatively simple structures have already shown so many emergent properties, colloidal chains with specific secondary and tertiary structures will definitely bring more interesting properties. Thus, it is another exciting area to be explored.
Out-of-equilibrium self-assembly systems endow materials with programmability in the time domain, leading to interactive, adaptive and autonomous behavior. This distinguishes out-of-equilibrium materials from classic stimuli-responsive materials switching among different equilibrium states.600 Current magnetic/electric field, hydrodynamic and light-sustained self-assembly can generate some superstructures with out-of-equilibrium characters. Overall, these superstructures are still far away from natural out-of-equilibrium self-assembly systems in a number of ways. For instance, out-of-equilibrium self-assembly systems in nature mainly rely on enzyme cascades and enzymatic reaction networks rather than relatively simple external fields to realize spatiotemporally controlled self-organization. In these natural systems, complicated signal activation, inhibition, autocatalysis, feedback and feedforward are involved.797–803 Using these enzyme cascades and enzymatic reaction networks, natural out-of-equilibrium self-assembly systems create much more complicated superstructures with high level programmability, reconfigurability and diversity. The gap between current manmade out-of-equilibrium self-assembled 1D structures and natural systems is huge in the aspects of either triggering and sustaining mechanisms or variety of assembly forms. However, this gap also points out the direction where we can discover new land for colloidal assembly.
Predicting and demonstrating emergent and enhanced collective properties, and pursuing new synthetic approaches to novel 1D colloidal chains complement and improve with each other. Early theoretical calculations about monodomain magnetic particles804 foretold the self-assembly of these particles into 1D nanochains by in situ formed magnetic dipole–dipole attractions. Theoretical prediction inspired chemists to develop bottom-up methods to prepare satisfactory NPs and confirm the calculation. After having obtained so many kinds of building blocks and assembling them into colloidal chains with geometric structures, these new geometric structures exhibit emergent and enhanced collective properties, some of them are never found in building blocks or bulk materials. These emergent properties attract scientists to further explore underlying theories to support these new discoveries.
Basically, the relationship between 1D colloidal structures and electromagnetic, electronic and magnetic properties has been relatively well explored and understood. However, the adaptive properties of 1D nanochains are very difficult to describe quantitatively, as these properties involve switching between assembly and disassembly states, transforming from one conformation to another, and multiparameter interactions among colloidal chains, external stimuli and media need to be considered. As a result, current research on adaptive properties of colloidal chains mainly focuses on qualitatively profiling the behavior of nanochains in a specific scenario. Even so, such behaviors and properties are already complicated enough to delineate.670,672
Although we did not outline every detailed aspect of the applications of 1D nanochains, comparing the structures of colloidal chains in the section of synthesis with applications, one can easily find that the structures of chains used for applications are much simpler. Fine structures such as orientation, sequence, and defined geometric construction are rarely taken into consideration in applications. The vast gap implies that these fancy 1D structures need to be connected to practical applications. Many studies stop after obtaining colloidal chains with elegant architectures, and characterizing one of their emergent and enhanced collective properties, which is by no means insignificant. However, maybe it is the time to do it reversely. As colloidal engineers, we can communicate with researchers in the fields of practical material applications, listen to their demands, and design the structure of colloidal chains based on their requirements. Meanwhile, we still need to develop scalable, practical procedures to assemble 1D colloidal chains with precisely controlled structures, and be ready for the challenging demands from practical applications.

Table Content:

Xinlong Fan
	Xinlong Fan earned his bachelor degree in 2010 and doctoral degree in 2016 at Northwestern Polytechnical University. There he focused on morphology regulation and formation mechanism of functional polymeric particles in emulsion system, and synthesis and application of thermally stable anion exchange resin under the supervision of Prof. Qiuyu Zhang. From 2016 to 2019, he worked as a postdoc in the Walther group at University of Freiburg on spatial and time-programmed dynamic self-assembly of colloids and responsive molecules. In 2019, he joined Stephen Mann group at University of Bristol to integrate dynamic self-assembly into protocells.

Andreas Walther
	Andreas Walther is a Gutenberg Research Professor for Macromolecular Materials and Systems at the University of Mainz in Germany. His research interests concentrate on self-assembly concepts inside and outside equilibrium for the design of bioinspired and life-inspired systems with active, adaptive and autonomous behavior. He is a two times ERC grantee, a founding PI of the DFG Cluster livMatS, and a former Fellow of the Freiburg and Strasbourg Institutes for Advanced Studies. He has published ca. 200 publications, and has received the Bayer Early Excellence in Science Award, the Reimund Stadler Award of the GDCh, and the Hanwha-Total IUPAC Award.

 	Fig. 1  With higher structural complexity come more properties and functionalities. When 0D colloids self-assemble into hierarchical 1D colloidal chains, and 1D colloidal chains further assemble into higher dimensional arrays and superstructures, more collective and emergent properties will appear, which lead to more functionalities.	 

 	Fig. 2  Scheme of structure of building blocks and strategies of fabricating 1D colloidal chains in this review. Basically, four main categories of preparing 1D colloidal chains are identified: field-directed assembly based on dipole–dipole interaction, template-directed, ligand-mediated method, and directional self-assembly of shape anisotropic building blocks. The last category is the newly emerged out-of-equilibrium assembly of colloids mimicking living out-of-equilibrium systems.

 	Fig. 4  Colloidal chains of magnetic NPs assembled through in situ formed dipole–dipole interactions in the absence of an external magnetic field. (a–a′) Compared to (a) conventional TEM, (a′) the in situ cryo-TEM image shows Fe3O4 NP chains with shorter lengths. (b) Ni7Co3 nanochains assemble into nanorings because of unsaturated magnetic dipoles. (c) Co NP-tipped nanorods and Co NPs co-assemble into block colloidal chains. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 81 Copyright 2004, American Chemical Society (ACS); (b) ref. 172 Copyright 2008, ACS; (c) ref. 171 Copyright 2014, ACS.	 

 	Fig. 5  Morphologies and structures of colloidal chains prepared in the presence of an external magnetic field. (a–a′) Comparing to the (a) monodisperse NPs prepared without external magnetic field, (a′) magnetic NPs obtained with an external magnetic field are polydisperse. (b–b′) Longer magnetic-field exposure time leads to longer 1D colloidal chains: (b) 85 min exposure time generates chains with ∼6 clusters, while (b′) longer exposure time prolongs the chain to ∼10–40 clusters. (c–c′) The rate of increasing the strength of the external magnetic field affects the sequence of colloidal chains when using polydisperse magnetic particles as building blocks: (c) slow increase results in block nanochains, and (c′) a fast increase results in statistical nanochains. (d) Cubic magnetic particles assemble into kinked chains in a high magnetic field. (e) Scheme and TEM image of single-stranded helices assembled from Fe3O4 nanocubes. (f–f′) Magnetic cubic chain shows reversible folding behavior programmed by an external magnetic field. (g–g′) Non-magnetic particles (green in scheme and microscope image) and magnetic particles (dark brown in scheme and microscope image) assemble into 1D colloidal chains with (g) binary colloids and (g′) ternary colloids (with third kind of non-magnetic particles shown in red in scheme and microscope image) in ferrofluid under external magnetic field. (h) Non-magnetic particles themselves can assemble into single component chains in ferrofluid in the presence of external magnetic field. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 299 Copyright 2018, ACS; (b) ref. 300 Copyright 2015, ACS; (c) ref. 301 Copyright 2015, ACS; (d) the term of Creative Commons Attribution-Non Commercial 3.0 Unported Licence, ref. 302 Copyright 2018, the authors, published by the Royal Society of Chemistry (RSC); (e) ref. 219 Copyright 2014, American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS); (f) the terms of Creative Commons-Attribution-NonCommercial 4.0 International-CC BY-NC 4.0, ref. 303 Copyright 2017, the authors, published by AAAS; (g) ref. 304 Copyright 2013, ACS; (h) ref. 305 Copyright 2018, Macmillan Publishers.	 

 	Fig. 6  Morphologies and structures of 1D colloidal chains prepared in the presence of an external electric field. (a) Phase diagram of ionic microgel assemblies in an AC electric field shows that the microgel only forms 1D strings in the presence of low effective volume fractions (Φeff). (b) Scheme and microscope image of alternatingly aligned, short binary nanochains after removing the electric field. (c–c′′) The geometry of ellipsoidal colloids influences their 1D superstructure of the assemblies. With increasing aspect ratio of the ellipsoidal colloids, (c) 1D strings, (c′) 1D tubular structures, and (c′′) 1D solid filaments were obtained. (d) Scheme and microscope image of head-to-tail connected 1D chains using Janus particles as building blocks. (e–e′) Salt concentration can control the structures of aligned Janus PS/Au ellipsoids in an AC field, they formed (e) staggered chains with low NaCl concentration (<1.5 mM) and (e′) stretched chain-link structures when the concentration was above 1.5 mM. (f) Optical image and scheme of a double helix chain using multi-patch particles as building blocks. (g) 1D lock-and-key colloidal chains aligned along the direction of the electrical field. (h) Optical image of microparticles aligned perpendicularly to a high frequency AC electric field. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 330 Copyright 2012, RSC; (b) the term of Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-NoDerivs 3.0 Unported License (https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/3.0/), ref. 337 Copyright 2012, the authors, published by Springer Nature; (c) the term of Creative Commons Attribution 4.0 International License (https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/), ref. 338 Copyright 2014, the authors, published by Springer Nature; (d) ref. 220 Copyright 2016, Macmillan Publishers; (e) ref. 339 Copyright 2015, Macmillan Publishers; (f) ref. 340 Copyright 2015, ACS; (g) ref. 341 Copyright 2016, ACS; (h) ref. 342 Copyright 2010, RSC.

 	Fig. 8  1D colloidal chains using peptides and proteins as templates. (a) By properly designing polypeptide and assembling the polypeptide into 1D fibrils, spatially precise repeating, positively charged histidine patches (dark orange bar in scheme) electrostatically attract negatively charged Au NPs, forming Au NP chains with defined interparticle distance. (b) The length of the aliphatic tail in peptide affects the pitch of the helical ribbon assembled from the peptide, therefore, adjusting the aliphatic tail can tune the pitch of chiral Au NP helices templated from the ribbon. (c) Scheme and TEM image of left-handed Au NP double helices, which were obtained by in situ reducing HAuCl4 during the assembly of amphiphilic peptides into 1D fibril templates. (d) Scheme and TEM image of 1D Au@α-synuclein NP chains induced by the amyloid fibrillation of α-synuclein. (e) Scheme and TEM image of highly oriented 1D Au NR chains assembled onto flagella via weak electrostatic interactions. (f) Scheme and TEM image of 1D co-assembly of Hcp1 pore protein and Au NPs via thiol- and dipole-induced self-assembly. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 410 Copyright 2009, Wiley-VCH; (b) ref. 153 Copyright 2017, ACS; (c) ref. 421 Copyright 2008, ACS; (d) ref. 434 Copyright 2011, Wiley-VCH; (e) ref. 435 Copyright 2021, Wiley-VCH; (f) ref. 440 Copyright 2015, Macmillan Publishers Limited.

 	Fig. 10  1D Colloidal structures fabricated in confining templates. (a–a′) Scheme and fluorescent image of array of 1D Ag chain formed on a glass substrate by micromoulding in capillaries. Scheme and microscope images of digital control over the (b–b′) width and (c) length of colloidal chains by adjusting the geometry of confining templates and the size of building blocks. (d–d′′) 1D linear colloidal chains with different sequences (green (G), blue (B), and red (R) in scheme and images) assembled by sequential capillarity-assisted particle assembly. (e) Capillary confinements between micro pillars induced the co-assembly of binary colloids to form 1D colloidal structures. (f) Scheme and SEM image of Au NP chains aligned along 1D PDMS wrinkles. (g) Scheme and TEM images of AuNPs@PS confined in an AAO nanochannel. By adjusting the ratio of channel diameter (Dc) and Au NP diameter (Ds), 1D Au NP chains with different configurations were generated. (h–h′′) Scheme and TEM images of helical Au NP chains templated by helical structures in block copolymer films. The chiral tartaric acid directs the achiral poly(1,4-butadiene)-block-poly(ethylene oxide) to assemble into a microphase separated structure. After removing the tartaric acid, Au NPs fill the residual space and form 1D chiral Au NP chains. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 458 Copyright 2002, Nature; (b) the term of Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial No Derivative Works 4.0 license, ref. 365 Copyright 2020, the authors, published by ACS; (c) ref. 367 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH; (d) the term of Creative Commons – Attribution-NonCommercial 4.0 International – CC BY-NC 4.0, ref. 450 Copyright 2016, the authors, published by AAAS; (e) ref. 449 Copyright 2017, ACS; (f) the term of Creative Commons – Attribution-NonCommercial 4.0 International – CC BY-NC 4.0, ref. 464 Copyright 2021, the authors, published by Springer Nature; (g) ref. 364 Copyright 2020, RSC and the Chinese Chemical Society; (h) ref. 456 Copyright 2017, ACS.	 

 	Fig. 11  Colloidal chains mediated by polymer ligands. (a–a′) Scheme and TEM image of 1D assembly of Au NP@PS-b-PAA core–shell particles assisted by the sphere-to-cylinder transformation of PS-b-PAA micelles. The core–shell particles energetically prefer to attach to the ends of a nanochain rather than its lateral sides. (b–b′) Scheme and TEM images of alternating colloidal chains by PEG-induced electrostatic self-assembly. (c–c′′) Scheme and SEM images of self-assembly of polymer-grafted nanocubes: (c′) nanocubes modified with PVP–thiol aggregate in an edge–edge (EE) configuration after solvent annealing of PS thin film where the nanocubes were embedded; (c′′) while nanocubes modified with PEG-alkanethiol ligands rearrange into a face–face (FF) configuration after both solvent and thermal annealing.  	Fig. 12  1D colloidal superstructures induced by small molecules and their assemblies. (a) Scheme of 1D Ag(I)-tiopronin sandwich-like complexes, and (a′) TEM image of Ag NP chains templated from the complexes. (b) Scheme of how isotropic electrostatic repulsion, van der Waals attraction, and anisotropic dipolar attraction regulate the self-assembly of charged particles; and (b′) TEM image of Au NP chains assembled by adjusting the ionic strength and polarity of the solvent. (c–c′′) Scheme and SEM images of Au NP chains assembled by anisotropic Debye length attraction induced by trace amount of water in ethanol. Diffusion-limited colloid aggregation (DLCA) results in branched aggregates, while reaction-limited colloid aggregation (RLCA) leads to more compact aggregates. (d–d′) Scheme and TEM image of 1D protein-jacketed superparamagnetic NPs (SNP) by Mg2+-mediated supramolecular polymerization of a NP-containing chaperonin protein (GroEL). (e–e′′) 1D chiral assemblies of gold nanorods induced by self-association of (e′) L- and (e′′) D-glutathione. (f–f′) Scheme and TEM image of 1D Au double helix triggered by the self-assembly of promesogenic compound (L) and liquid crystal-coated Au NPs. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 163 Copyright 2012, Wiley-VCH; (b) ref. 505 Copyright 2008, Wiley-VCH; (c) ref. 506 Copyright 2015, ACS; (d) ref. 157 Copyright 2015, ACS; (e) ref. 507 Copyright 2017, ACS; (f) ref. 508 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH.	 

 	Fig. 13  1D colloidal chains induced by depletion attraction. (a–a′′) Scheme and optical images of (a) cross-chains, (a′) ladder chains and (a′′) tilted ladder chains assembled from particles with different length width ratio. The assembly was triggered by Pluronic F127. (b) Scheme and confocal laser scanning microscope (CLSM) image of 1D cone-shaped particle chains where PEG 600 kDa was used as depletant. (c) Scheme and TEM images of 1D two-stranded filaments assembled from modified hexagon DNA origami, the assembly resulted from shape complementarity of the DNA origami. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 563 Copyright 2020, Springer Nature; (b) ref. 203 Copyright 2016, Wiley-VCH; (c) ref. 158 Copyright 2015, AAAS.	 

 	Fig. 14  Hydrogen bond and DNA-mediated binding-directed 1D self-assembly of building blocks. (a) Scheme and TEM image of end-to-end assembly of Au NRs induced by the hydrogen bonding between UPy coated on NRs. (b) Scheme and optical images of step-growth alternating colloidal polymerization of two kinds of building blocks with complementary divalent patches (red and green tips in scheme). (c) Scheme and TEM image of colloidal oligomers formed by two kinds of divalent programmable atom-like NP A and B, where the bonding domains of x and y are complementary to x′ and y′ respectively. (d) Scheme and microscope images of reconfigurable thermally reversible transition among singlets, 1D chains and 2D bilayer structures using DNA-patched Janus particles as building blocks. (e) Scheme and TEM images of switchable 1D DNA origami superstructures: the assembly was induced by the complementary connector pairs at both ends of the DNA origami, while the disassembly can be achieved by thermal and chemical switching.  	Fig. 15  1D colloidal chains assembled by host–guest attraction.  	Fig. 16  Solvophobic attraction induced self-assembly of building blocks into 1D structures. (a–a′) Scheme and SEM image of self-assembly of Au NRs with PS at both ends by adding water into DMF. (b) Scheme of the formation of divalent building block, which has two sticky patches on opposing sides of the core and a repulsive patch orthogonally surrounding the core, and Janus structure with attractive and repulsive patches on opposing sides by selectively adding solvent; (b′–b′′) TEM images of supracolloidal polymer chains by solvent-induced assembly of divalent particles and Janus particles. (c–c′′′) Scheme and TEM images of 1D superstructures with side-by-side (c–c′) and end-to-end (c′′–c′′′) configuration of triblock co-micelles. The triblock co-micelles contain a nonpolar, hydrophobic central segment and two polar terminal segments. When adding a mixture of hexane and isopropanol, a non-solvent for the central segments, the co-micelles assembled side-by-side. While adding hexane, a non-solvent for the terminal segments, the co-micelles assembled end-to-end. (d–d′) Scheme and TEM image of polymerization-induced particle-assembly of triblock polymers. Grafting a HPMA block onto the PDMA-b-PBzMA colloidal atom generates two patches on opposing side of the colloid. The solvent for the polymerization directly induced the self-assembly. (e–e′) Scheme and TEM image of Au NPs homogeneously functionalized with a mixture of 11-mercaptoundecanoic acid and 1-dodecanethiol assembling to chain-like structures. When dispersing the Au NPs in water, hydrophobic ligands redistributed on the particle surface to minimize their contact with water, and the resulting hydrophobic patches induced the self-assembly. This figure is adapted with permission from: (a) ref. 582 Copyright 2007, Nature; (b) ref. 72 Copyright 2013, Macmillan Publishers; (c) ref. 588 Copyright 2015, AAAS; (d) ref. 589 Copyright 2020, RSC; (e) ref. 590 Copyright 2014, ACS.	 

 	Fig. 17  1D particle chains in out-of-equilibrium self-assembly states. (a–a′) 1D tubular assemblies of (a) mono- and (a′) multi-component building blocks assembled in a rotating fluid.  	Fig. 27  1D colloidal chains as catalysts.  	Fig. 28  1D colloidal chains used as energy materials.  	Fig. 29  1D colloidal chains for biomedical applications. (a) Scheme and TEM image of the interactions of FeO NP@C nanochains with E. coli, and (a′) comparing to the magnetic nanochains without wash steps after each treatment, the nanochains with wash steps have higher loss of E. coli viability. (b) Scheme and TEM image of vincristine-loaded superparamagnetic anisotropic nano-assemblies (VCR-SANs). The VCR-SANs have a much longer mean residence time (MRT) in plasma compared to VCR and VCR-loaded isotropic nano-assemblies (VCR-SINs). (c) Compared to monodisperse spheres (S-MNPs-PPR) and clusters (C-MNPs-PPR) of Mn–Zn ferrite loading Cyclo(RGDyK) (PPR), 1D worm-like structures (W-MNPs-PPR) possess a higher relaxivity coefficient (r2), therefore show better negative contrast effects. (d–d′) Scheme of polydopamine-coated Au NPs and nanochains, and the comparison of intracellular dark field scattering images of HeLa cells incubated for 24 h with unassembled NPs and nanochains. (e) Scheme of Au NP chains coated with SiO2 and the H2O2-induced shift of chain plasmon resonance. (e′) Combined with laser radiation, such Au NP chains can effectively reduce the relative tumor volume, showing better tumor-microenvironment-activated theranostics behavior.
